# Drystone, Part 2: The Certifiable Design

`Status: beta, rebuild complete. Build-against shape complete across §0 through §10 and Appendices A through F, with the final consistency pass done: internal cross-references resolve, the §0 map matches the section structure, Appendix E's beat references are reconciled to the final subsection numbering, and every inline citation resolves to an Appendix F entry. Open where the spec says so, the load-bearing completeness beam, the byte-level [gates-release] encodings, and the committed BLAKE3 suite's end-to-end re-proof of §4 (all Appendix B). Supersedes p10 as the authoritative Part 2 on confirmation.`

`Resolution: library, the complete reference and the coherence authority for the Drystone set. For shorter tellings see the coffee-shop overview, then the elevator pitch.`

## 0. Map

`A per-section index with scope, dependencies, and orthogonality, maintained as sections change. Section numbers run per part, and cross-part references name the part: Part 1 holds §1 through §3 (positioning, the principles, and their corroboration), and Part 2 holds its own §1 (this introduction) and §3 onward (the mechanics), so Part 1 §3 and this Part 2 §3 are distinct. Each entry states what the section covers and, where it matters, what it depends on and what it is orthogonal to, so a reader or a later fold can pull the relevant section without dragging in the unrelated ones.`

- **§2. What makes this a system of peers: the diagnostic that orders.** The mechanical test that orders the whole specification: count the adjudication loci, since a principal is a locus that can adjudicate where a sensor only senses and relays, so a network can be any-to-any wired and still be a centralized apparatus wearing a sensor mesh as a costume. This is why rights are specified separately from resources (§5) and why the label-not-enforce posture (§8) is load-bearing. Frames everything below.

- **§3. Protocol overview and the running example.** The two kinds of state a Group moves (history in the data plane, governance facts in the control plane), the Group-versus-scope distinction, and the running example the rest of the specification demonstrates on: the fixed cast and its resources (§3.1) and the helper ecosystem of node roles (§3.2), both additive to the normative text.

- **§4. Data Model.** The representation layer: cryptographic foundation (§4.1), identifiers and derivations (§4.2), the signed message as the unit of history (§4.3), integrity-and-ordering versus authorship-and-standing as two distinct guarantees (§4.4), the multi-client fold, where client-count and device-count are not persona-count (§4.5), and canonical-on-the-wire encoding with free local storage (§4.6). *Depends on:* nothing internal; it is the base. *Orthogonal to:* the governance semantics of §7 (this is representation, §7 is resolution).

- **§5. Identity, Principals, Rights, Roles, and Capabilities.** Who acts and with what standing: the two equalities and two inequalities (§5.0), the only-canonical-state-is-local claim (§5.1), the principal, client, and persona model (§5.2), the inherent rights floor of tenure, voice, and exit (§5.3), resources versus grants (§5.4), Group Roles and capabilities across the governance and data-access planes (§5.5), weight anchored to personhood (§5.6), membership, standing, and revocation (§5.7, §5.8), exitability (§5.9), the Group as a principal (§5.10), and read-scoped content keys that cryptographically enforce the read role (§5.11). *Depends on:* §4 (identifiers). *Orthogonal to:* transport §6 (standing does not depend on how bytes travel). §5.9 exitability is the backstop §7.6 relies on.

- **§6. Transport and Delivery: the Three Planes.** How bytes move and who can see what: the two identity planes (§6.1), the two-layer encryption stack (§6.2), the Delivery Fabric versus the Group (§6.3), the metadata floor (§6.4), the three planes Carriage, Durability, and Presence (§6.5 through §6.7), gap-aware history convergence and the content-blind history store (§6.8), discovery and interaction tiers (§6.9), the gossip overlay realization (§6.10), the two deployment modes (§6.11), and real-time media (§6.12). *Depends on:* §4 (the message), §5 (who is a member). *Orthogonal to:* the governance-conflict resolution of §7 (delivery is mechanism-neutral about what it carries). §6.8 gap-aware convergence is the completeness machinery the §7.3 beam leans on.

- **§7. Synchronization and Governance-Conflict Resolution.** The governance machinery, the largest section and internally the most separable.

  - **§7.1, §7.2.** Data-model commitment, then the mechanism-neutral, normative grant-and-revocation interface.

  - **§7.3. Governance facts are entries, not mutations** (the core). §7.3.1 the total order (causal and cryptographic, never temporal); §7.3.2 what conflicts; §7.3.3 the declarative snapshot as cache and the read-and-enforce line; §7.3.4 sign-the-state-not-the-authorship; §7.3.5 the membership ceiling; §7.3.6 decision versus enactment; §7.3.7 the now; §7.3.8 the finality gate; §7.3.9 principal recovery and break-glass (a stub). *Depends on:* §5 (standing), §4 (facts). The whole §7.3 run rests on the completeness beam (Appendix B).

  - **§7.4.** Freshness, no false current; §7.4.1 the governed false-positive tolerance; §7.4.2 the two MLS recovery hazards the corroboration model dissolves; §7.4.3 the governance-generation stamp that lets a data-plane entry self-locate against the authority chain.

  - **§7.5.** Attributable acceptance and the regress-free fold; §7.5.2 breaks the authority-ordering regress and closes the resolution input set.

  - **§7.6. The reconcile hard-stop and re-formation fork** (conflict handling). §7.6.1 the two-member escalation set; §7.6.2 one primitive, three arities; §7.6.3 MLS subordinate; §7.6.4 a ban is a fork; §7.6.5 the three registers (mute, governance, fork); §7.6.6 fork placement; §7.6.7 the epoch roll is the audience split; §7.6.8 permanent-both and cheap merge; §7.6.9 posture dials and the protocol and product division; §7.6.10 re-composition as a view and the adjudication rubric. *Depends on:* §7.3 (the order and the ceiling), §5.9 (exitability). *Orthogonal to:* §7.9 scaling.

  - **§7.7, §7.8.** Dataplane history modes (forward-only versus Willow-mutable) and side histories.

  - **§7.9. Scaling: commits, folds, and the relocation of ordering cost.** *Depends on:* §7.3.1 and §7.3.2 (order-independence) and the Appendix B beam; §7.7 and §7.8 (catch-up). *Orthogonal to:* §7.6 conflict-handling; a scaling change rarely touches conflict semantics.

- **§8. Security Considerations.** Forward secrecy and durable history are not in tension (§8.1); the honesty boundaries the spec still carries (§8.2). *Depends on:* §6 (the stack), §7 (the governance model).

- **§9. Interoperability and Conformance.** What two implementations must share to interoperate.

- **§10. Substrate Requirements and Reference Realizations.** Requirement-first mapping onto the messaging backplane (§10.2), transport and overlay (§10.3), and primitives (§10.4), closed by the dependency-versus-realization ledger (§10.5). *Depends on:* §4 through §7 (it states what each layer requires, then names one conforming realization).

- **§11. Large-Group Scaling, Dormancy, and Re-entry.** Cost scales on the live set, not the roster (§11.4); scaled post-compromise security, strict below 250 and opportunistic above (§11.5); the hot/cold Group split and liveness-driven migration (§11.6); the two-part re-entry credential (§11.7); lineage-scoped bans on the single governance chain (§11.8); delivery as a store-and-forward/swarm race (§11.9); the encryption posture and the two forces (§11.9.1), the optional public-projection cache (§11.9.2), and the experimental public-by-default regime above ~7k (§11.9.3); tiered performance with a buildable experiment matrix (§11.10, §11.10.1); open items and the two unearned measurements (§11.11); the empirical basis (§11.13) and the standing research obligation (§11.14). *Depends on:* §5, §6, §7.

- **Appendix A. Alternatives Considered.** A.1 decentralized MLS and the forward-secrecy cost of center-free ordering.

- **Appendix B. Open Questions.** The tracked residuals. Leads with the single `Load-bearing, unearned` beam (completeness-ahead), then the governance `[confirm]` presets (enactment dial, the now's schema, tiebreak and weighting defaults), then the naming, hash, wire-encoding, substrate, and security items. Referenced by §7.3.3, §7.3.7, §7.3.8, and §7.9.

- **Appendix C. Consolidated Prior Art and Positioning.** C.1 the data layer, C.2 the governance and resolution layer (same skeleton, opposite spine), C.3 the cryptographic group-state layer, C.4 the governance-as-protocol frontier, C.5 the cross-disciplinary grounding.

- **Appendix D. Term lattice and invariants** (the vocabulary of record): entities and genus, the hosting chain, MLS-carried terms, lineage and the count, the four properties and the non-property, the bundle, relation, and participant senses, the invariants of record, and the delivery-plane vocabulary.

- **Appendix E. The running example, chained.** The §3.1 cast walked through the Group's whole life as one arc (E1 formation to E10 re-composition), each beat listing the sections it demonstrates and linked to the in-body beat there. Additive, no new normative content.

- **Appendix F. References and external primaries.** Every external primary the spec cites, grouped by kind and each with a precise locator, so every inline citation resolves to one entry, reconciled against the inline citations in both directions.

---

## 1. Introduction and scope

This part is the "what." It specifies the mechanics an implementation is built and validated against, where Part 1 argues the principles those mechanics discharge. Each section names the Part 1 principle or principles it `Realizes`, and every normative clause states why, what a MUST secures and what a MUST NOT prevents, tracing to the principle it serves rather than being argued here. Normative keywords are **MUST**, **SHOULD**, and **MAY** (BCP 14).

Two companion documents bound this one. **Part 1** carries the principles and their corroboration, and a `Realizes: P-X` tag is the link from a mechanism here to the imperative it serves there. The **conventions and decisions** document is the vocabulary and decisions authority both parts defer to: every load-bearing term, group and Group, role and Group Role, principal, persona, peer, and scope, has one working definition there, and this part uses each term in that sense. Where this part and a companion could collide on a section number, a cross-part reference names the part, so "Part 1 §3" and this part's §3 never read as one pointer.

### 1.1. The status legend

Each normative claim carries exactly one status flag from the canonical ladder, which the conventions and decisions document records as the single set both parts defer to and which is reproduced here so this part reads on its own:

- **`Verified`**: demonstrated against real crypto or real transport in a running reference implementation.

- **`Verified-RFC`**: verified against a normative primary source, for example RFC 9420 or RFC 9750.

- **`Modeled`**: a reference model exists and is reasoning-complete, but is not yet backed by real crypto.

- **`Measured`**: an empirical or benchmark measurement.

- **`Established`**: an established result in the literature, or an inherited primitive used as-is.

- **`Design`**: specified but unproven, a design decision the part commits to.

- **`Synthesis`**: a claim assembled across several sources rather than resting on a single citation.

- **`Load-bearing, unearned`**: a property the design leans on that is not yet earned or proven, flagged rather than smoothed over.

- **`[gates-release]`**: a byte-level encoding or specification item that must be pinned before a publication-final release and before two implementations can interoperate.

- **`[confirm]`**: rests on an external fact not yet independently verified.

Two linkage markers sit alongside the ladder and are not status flags: `Realizes: P-X`, a section naming the Part 1 principle it discharges, and the `P-X` principle codes themselves (`P-Local-Truth`, `P-Knowable-Truth`, `P-Peer-Equality`, `P-Durable-Enablement`).

### 1.2. Two maturity layers in this part

This part specifies one design at two maturity levels, and stating which is which keeps a reader from hearing a proven claim and a designed one in the same voice. The message, history, and transport layer (§4, §6) is matured from a reference implementation that is `Verified` on real crypto and real iroh transport. Within §6, the MLS-architecture claims, the two identity planes, the two-layer encryption stack, the DS and AS trust model, and the metadata exposure, are `Verified-RFC` against RFC 9420 and RFC 9750, and the iroh transport-plane claims are verified against iroh 1.0, wire-and-API-stable: public-key identity, direct-first delivery with blind relay fallback, and post-handshake authentication. The items left `[confirm]` are confined to the layers iroh's 1.0 guarantee does not cover, the separately-versioned `iroh-gossip` crate and the address-lookup crates. The governance-conflict-resolution layer (§7.3 through §7.5) is matured from a `Design`-stage draft whose reasoning is complete but whose wire encodings and one comparative dependency are not yet pinned. The committed hash is BLAKE3, which §7 is designed on and onto which §4 (today proven on SHA-256) is re-based, so SHA-256 is the legacy side; proving §4 out end-to-end on BLAKE3, re-deriving the signature proofs and re-running the conformance suite, is a real open item carried in Appendix B rather than papered over.

### 1.3. Naming: center-free, and vendor-neutral tags

Drystone is a **center-free peer protocol**. *Peer-to-peer* describes its transport (§6) accurately and this part uses it there, in the sense the conventions document fixes for peer, an edge relation rather than a kind of principal. This part does not call the protocol *serverless*, a term that now names managed ephemeral compute, nearly the inverse of what is meant. The load-bearing property is not topology but where adjudication lives (§2): no node holds privileged or canonical authority. Where precision is needed this part says *center-free*, and where it means the wiring it says *peer-to-peer*.

The reference implementation this part is matured from carried a historical brand in some signed wire constants, for example a domain-separation tag namespace. Those values are shown where they are what was proven, but they are the reference profile, not the protocol: Drystone requires a versioned, domain-separated tag and does not mandate that string. Defining the vendor-neutral tag namespace and re-proving the rename, since the tag is signed over and changing it reopens the signature proofs, is an Appendix B item.

## 2. What makes this a system of peers: the diagnostic that orders the part

A network of many nodes with any-to-any connectivity is **not** automatically a distributed system of peers. The distinction this specification turns on is not topological; it is about **where adjudication lives.** A **principal is a locus that can adjudicate**: it holds genuine authority over some domain that other principals must respect, not merely a node that can sense, store, and relay. A node that only senses and relays, its decisions made elsewhere, is a **sensor**, however well connected it is. To say a system is one *of peers* is to say its principals stand in **peer relation**: each is a locus the others must respect, none a center the others must obey. `Synthesis.`

This is also why the protocol is named *center-free* rather than *peer-to-peer*: peer-to-peer is a true statement about the wiring and a misleading one about the authority. A blockchain is peer-to-peer and builds a single canonical chain, and that global apex is precisely what a center-free design refuses. The word that names the property is not in the topology. `Synthesis.`

The cautionary archetype is a national economic-planning network of the 1970s with tens of thousands of local terminals and any-terminal-to-any-terminal wiring, whose adjudication nonetheless all funneled to a single apex: thousands of nodes, **zero peers in the sense that matters.** Its terminals sensed, reported, and received instructions, and none adjudicated. **The wires lie; the authority topology tells the truth.** The diagnostic that follows is mechanical: **count the adjudication loci.** If the answer is one, or a small apex, the system is a centralized decision apparatus wearing a distributed sensor mesh as a costume, regardless of how its packets flow. `Synthesis.`

This is why Drystone specifies **rights separately from resources** (§5) and cannot let either be read off a network diagram: a **resource** (this device can sense, store, relay, or compute) is visible in the plumbing, but a **right** (this principal's authority over some domain must be respected) is visible only in the governance. It is also why the part is **ordered** as it is, defining the **principal** as a locus of adjudication and the **persona** as its human-representing kind (§5.2), then the rights floor (§5.3), then the mechanics that keep adjudication distributed (§7, §8). And it is why the **label-not-enforce** posture (§8) is load-bearing rather than cosmetic: enforcement relocates adjudication to whoever enforces, quietly converting principals into sensors, while labeling leaves adjudication with the principal and propagates only information. Each enforcement hook looks locally reasonable, which is exactly how a system of peers can **degrade into a sensor mesh** over time with no one deciding to centralize. Keeping adjudication at the edge is the protocol's job, and surfacing the hard case to a human (§7.6) is how it does so without pretending rules resolve everything. `Synthesis.`

## 3. Protocol overview and the running example

A **persona** is represented by a root key pair standing for a single cryptographic lineage, and it is the escalation path for the social-utility judgments bearing on the canonical data store held with respect to it (`P-Local-Truth`). Personae participate in **Groups**: entitlement-and-governance units that hold shared state, each realized over an MLS group (§6, §10.2). Within a Group two kinds of state move, and the split between them is load-bearing throughout the specification.

- **History**: the content personae author, carried as signed, hash-chained entries. This is the data plane, its unit defined in §4 and its convergence in §6.8.

- **Governance facts**: signed, append-only entries recording who may do what, that is admit, expel, grant, revoke, and amend. Authority is a deterministic fold over these entries rather than a mutable state (§7.3). This is the control plane.

A Group's **scope** is wider than its membership. Scope is the extent of exposure and processing for the Group's traffic, the reach of routing and metadata beyond the entitled members, so a node that carries or triggers on a Group's traffic without holding its keys, a relay, a store-and-forward meer, or a push-notify node, sits in the Group's scope without being a member of the Group. Governance and entitlement facts attach to the **Group**; exposure and routing-reach facts attach to the **scope**. The two terms, and the lowercase-**group** social sense beneath the capital-G Group, are fixed in §5.0 and §5.2.

A persona is one or more **devices**, each a keypair, acting under a single **lineage**, and receivers fold the devices back to one persona so that membership and thresholds are counted by persona and never by device (§4.5, §5.2). Personae reach each other over an encrypted QUIC transport with relay fallback, where a relay forwards opaque frames and need not read content (§6). When two views diverge, **range-based reconciliation** finds the difference and exchanges the missing entries (§6.8): history converges by hash-chain replay, and governance converges by a timestamp-free causal-and-cryptographic resolution order (§7.3.1). A genuine membership contradiction is **not** auto-merged; it hard-stops and escalates to the people, and a minority's sanctioned exit is a clean, attributable **re-formation fork** (§7.6).

§2 gives the diagnostic that orders the specification, the count of adjudication loci that separates a system of peers from a sensor mesh wearing distributed wiring. The sections after this one specify each piece: §4 is the data model, §5 is identity, rights, and capabilities, §6 is transport, §7 is synchronization and governance-conflict resolution, §8 is security, §9 is interoperability and conformance, and §10 consolidates the substrate requirements and their reference realizations, separating each *requirement* from its current *realization*. Appendix C consolidates the prior art each layer builds on.

### 3.1. The running example: a cast the rest of the specification demonstrates on

The sections that follow carry their definitions and their normative clauses in full, and those stand on their own. Laid over them is a running example, a small fixed cast that recurs so each mechanism can be shown in place, and above all so each **MUST** and **MUST NOT** can be seen biting on a named actor rather than on "a node." The example is additive: a beat never carries an obligation in a clause's stead, it shows the clause landing. The scattered beats chain into one continuous arc in **Appendix E**, which a reader who wants the whole journey at once should read. The cast is closed, and no beat anywhere introduces an actor or resource not named here, because a closed cast is what keeps the arc coherent across the whole document.

**Personae and their clients.**

- **Alice**, a persona, running two clients, a **phone** and a **laptop**, both under one key lineage. She holds a **moderator Group Role Set**. She is the worked case for one persona across many clients, for a Group Role Set layered over the flat rights floor, and for authority that is concentrated yet revocable.

- **Bob**, a persona, running one client, a **phone**. He is the worked case for the bare floor, and he is the member later banned who continues in his own lineage, since a ban is a forced fork.

- **Dave**, a persona, running two clients, a **phone** and a **tablet**, his phone offline for a stretch. He is the worked case for stale-but-honest convergence, for mobile wake, and for a fork bystander who lands where his own choice puts him.

- **Erin**, a persona, running one client, a **phone**, admitted alongside Dave. She is the worked case for equal branches in individual outcome: at the fork she lands with Bob's lineage where Dave lands with the Group, two opposite yet equally legitimate choices the substrate does not make for them, and at the later re-composition, having been banned in the meantime by Bob's lineage, she stays excluded by the floor while Bob is re-admitted by an explicit governed act.

The running example also uses two helper-node instances, **Carol's node** and **the history store**, whose roles sit in the fixed node vocabulary of §3.2. Between them they are the worked case for capability-is-not-authority (§5.4, §5.5, Part 1 §2.7), at the two confidentiality levels §3.2 separates: Carol's node reads clear text within a delegated scope, the history store holds none.

**Resources.**

- **The Group**: Alice, Bob, and shortly Dave and Erin, with its **append-only governance log**, the fact set the fold runs over.

- **The delivery fabric**: the nodes that carry frames, a superset of the Group within which Carol's node and the history store sit.

- **A k-of-n threshold** the Group sets for its membership and revocation decisions.

- **Alice's moderator Group Role Set**, which makes `floor + [roles] + [capabilities] + [resources]` concrete against Bob, who holds the floor alone and counts for exactly the same one unit of weight.

That is the demonstrative cast. The node roles it and every other section draw on are fixed next, in §3.2.

### 3.2. The helper ecosystem: a fixed cast of node roles

The demonstrative cast above is closed; so is the set of **node roles** that carry, hold, and signal a Group's traffic without governing it. These roles recur across the specification, above all in §6, and naming them once here lets every later section reference a role by name rather than re-describing it. The set below is the whole of it, and no section introduces a helper role not named here, which is the same fixed-cast discipline the personae follow.

One invariant binds every role, and it is why they are grouped. Each is a **scope** participant, not a Group member (§3), so each holds a capability or offers a resource and **none holds standing**: a helper cannot foreclose on a persona, remove a member, or govern, and each is revocable exactly as it was admitted or its use withdrawn. What separates the roles is the **plane** they serve (§6) and how much they can **see**, and those two are largely independent.

- **Relay** (carriage, §6.5). Forwards opaque frames between peers that cannot reach each other directly. It sees endpoint identifiers, timing, and size, never content, and is never required to read what it carries.

- **Swarm node** (carriage, §6.10). A participant in the gossip overlay that forwards along a self-healing broadcast tree while holding no global membership. Like a relay it carries frames it need not read, and it differs in forwarding by epidemic overlay rather than point to point.

- **Meer** (durability, §6.6). A store-and-forward node that holds sealed frames for a recipient who is offline until that recipient fetches them, then may drop them. It sees ciphertext and delivery metadata, never content, and its horizon is the in-flight message rather than deep history.

- **Durable history store** (durability, §6.6, §6.8.5). A node that retains a Group's deep history as sealed blobs and reconciles them over a content-free envelope, serving ciphertext spans a member unwraps and verifies for itself. It holds **no clear text**, and its horizon is long-term retention rather than in-flight delivery.

- **Push notifier** (presence, §6.7). A node that tells a recipient to look, a minimal wake signal. It sees that traffic exists and for whom to raise the signal, never content.

- **Read/search helper** (read scope, §5.5, §5.11, §6.6). A node admitted by delegation to a read scope, holding a read capability so that **within that scope** it holds clear text and can search and replay readable content. It is the one helper role that sees plaintext, it sees only the scope it was granted, and it still holds no standing.

Every role but the read/search helper is **content-blind**, which is the design's default: carriage, durability, and presence are provided by nodes that never see plaintext, and clear text reaches a helper only through an explicit, attenuating read-scope delegation. §6 specifies each role on its plane, and Carol's node and the history store (§3.1) are the running example's instances of the read/search-helper and durable-history-store roles.

## 4. Data model

`Realizes: P-Local-Truth, P-Knowable-Truth`

This is the representation layer: what is hashed, signed, and reconciled, and what is left free. It is the base the rest of the part rests on and is orthogonal to the governance semantics of §7, which resolve what these representations mean.

### 4.1. Cryptographic foundation

A device holds a signature keypair, its public half the verifying identity and its secret half the signer. A **lineage identifier** names a persona, and the persona's clients, across one or more devices, act under that one lineage (§5.2). A signature **MUST** verify against the author's published verifying key before any other check, because a check run on an unverified message would be trusting unauthenticated input, and a valid signature is **necessary but not sufficient**, since standing (§5, §7) is also required.

*Running example: when Bob's laptop receives Alice's post, it verifies her signature against her published key before any other check, because acting first would trust bytes whose author is not yet established (beat E2).*

The reference implementation uses **Ed25519** (RFC 8032, deterministic, 64-byte signatures) and **SHA-256** (32-byte digests). `Verified` against real Ed25519 over live iroh: a forged message is rejected as a bad signature on every receiver, including a NAT'd peer. A production profile **MAY** select a different suite, but the suite is part of the versioned wire profile and **MUST NOT** be silently negotiated down, because a silent downgrade is a downgrade attack: an adversary that could force a weaker suite unnoticed would undo the profile's guarantees. The abstract signature and hash requirements, and why Ed25519 and the SHA-256-versus-BLAKE3 choice are the references, are consolidated in §10.4.

### 4.2. Identifiers and derivations

Wire identifiers are a hash over a **tagged** pre-image, `tag = version ‖ domain-separator`, so one identifier kind can never collide with another's input, and an implementation **MUST** derive these identically because they are the interop anchor.

| identifier | pre-image (structure) |
|---|---|
| lineage genesis | `H(tag["lineage-genesis"] ‖ lineage_id)` |
| group genesis | `H(tag["group-genesis"] ‖ group_id)` |
| group gossip topic | `H(tag["group-topic"] ‖ group_id)` |
| content id | `H(canonical{group_id, regime, author_id, content})` |

`Verified`: the three tagged wire derivations are conformance-tested canonical functions, byte-identical to the proving spike. The reference profile's tag strings carry a historical brand namespace, and **Drystone normatively requires a versioned, domain-separated tag and does not mandate those strings** (§1.3, Appendix B).

**The gossip topic is a realization lever on scope, not the model.** The table derives one gossip topic per Group from the Group ID, which is a reasonable default and what the reference profile does, but it is a **realization choice, not the model**. Scope is the whole envelope of exposure and processing, the Delivery Fabric as a whole (§6.3): gossip, direct connections, relays, meers, and push notifiers, and the metadata each sees. The gossip topic is one large contributor to that envelope, since it governs who is in the dissemination swarm, but a relay in the path, a meer holding sealed bytes, or a push notifier each adds to scope independently of the topic, and seeding the topic from a value shared across several Groups widens scope past any one Group. What the model **requires** is only that the seed be high-entropy so the topic is not guessable, and reading "the reference profile keys the topic from the Group ID" as "scope is Group-specific" is the realization-for-requirement slide §10 exists to prevent. `Design.`

**The content-id pre-image carries no timestamp.** Per Part 1 §2.0.1 a wall-clock value is an uncorroborable assertion and **MUST NOT** appear in any identity, ordering, or authority-bearing computation, so a content id binds *what* and *where*, that is group, regime, author, and content, and never *when*. An application that wishes to record a claimed time does so as ordinary payload content, an explicitly author-asserted value, never a protocol fact and never an input to the content id. `Design`; the canonical content-id encoding is `[gates-release]` (Appendix B).

A scope's gossip-topic seed **MUST** be high-entropy or salted rather than a guessable human handle, because otherwise an adversary computes the topic and joins or observes (the leak bound is characterized in §8).

### 4.3. The signed message: the unit of history

The history unit binds author, position, branch, and payload so a message cannot be replayed onto another branch or position. The signed pre-image in the reference profile:

```
signing_bytes = "msg-v1" ‖ branch(32) ‖ seq(LE u64) ‖ author_id_bytes ‖ 0x00 ‖ payload
```

A receiver **MUST** recompute the pre-image and verify the signature against the author's key, because a signature over a pre-image the receiver did not reconstruct proves nothing about the fields the receiver then reads. `Verified`: the real message traveled live iroh-gossip and verified against a real backfill import, an honest member's message accepted and a forged one rejected. Position is `seq`, a per-branch counter and not a clock, so ordering is structural rather than temporal, consistent with Part 1 §2.0.1.

*Running example: Alice's post is the unit of history, and before accepting it Bob's laptop recomputes the pre-image from these signing fields and checks the signature against her key (beat E2).*

### 4.4. Integrity-and-ordering versus authorship-and-standing: two distinct guarantees

These are kept strictly separate, and conflating them is the central honesty error the protocol forbids.

- **Integrity and ordering (structural).** A branch is a sequence chained by `hash = H(prev ‖ seq(LE) ‖ payload)`, and a receiver **MUST** reject a branch with a broken chain or non-contiguous sequence numbers. This proves in-transit integrity and contiguous ordering, **not** who wrote it.

- **Authorship and standing (authority).** The signature (§4.3) plus standing (§5, §7). A receiver **MUST** apply both, and **integrity alone MUST NOT be treated as authorization**, because a well-formed branch from a non-member would otherwise be honored as if it were authorized.

`Verified`: a valid-chain branch from a non-member is accepted by the structural check but rejected by the authority check as an unauthorized author. This separation is what makes a branch trustworthy: the hash chain proves it was not tampered in transit, and only signature and standing prove it may be there.

*Running example: a valid-chain frame reaching Bob from a non-member passes his structural check but fails the standing check, so integrity alone does not authorize it (beat E2).*

### 4.5. Multi-client fold: client-count and device-count are not persona-count

A **persona** (the human layer's manifestation, §5.2) is rooted in a **cryptographic key pair**, and that key pair is the root of a **cryptographic lineage**: each of the persona's devices, and each **client** (group-member software, §5.2) hosted on them, carries a membership key that **descends from the persona's key pair by signed credential**. Lineage here is literal, the concrete chain of signatures by which a client's membership key is provably tied back to its rooting persona, not an abstract tier.

Receivers **MUST** fold by following each client's lineage back to its root, so every client whose lineage roots at the same persona counts as **that one persona**, however many clients run on however many devices, because standing is counted per persona and counting per client or device would let more devices buy more standing. A scope's topic carries many such lineages, and the fold is what every participant computes identically to agree on the member list and on **lineage-rooted thresholds** (§7.2), which count **one persona per rooting key pair** and never clients or devices. `Verified`: one human's two devices fold to one persona, and all participants agree on the folded count.

*Running example: Alice's phone and laptop are two clients under one lineage that fold to one persona, so she is one member counting for one unit of weight however many devices she runs (beat E1).*

#### 4.5.1. Per-client authorship, the per-client logical clock, and the principal as its own CA

Clients are **not** a shared key. Each **client** (§5.2: software on a device that is a member of a Group, one MLS leaf, one signature key, one credential) is an independent signer with its own signature key, and an assertion is **authored and signed per client**: the envelope carries `author_client` (the signing identity the signature verifies against), `author_device` (the device hosting it), and `author_principal` (the principal the client is credentialed to). The client-to-principal binding is a **credential**, not key-sharing. This is the only model consistent with the group-key layer, which **represents each client as its own member** (the MLS member is the client or leaf, and a device may run more than one) and which Drystone requires not to share one signature key across a principal's clients in a group, so "these clients are the same person" is necessarily an **identity-layer credential policy** and not a key-layer fact. This is the mechanism behind the governance-integrity spine (§5.2): because each client is a distinct member but governance counts **personae**, a persona's clients and devices are resolved by lineage to one persona before any quorum is counted, so more clients or devices never buy more weight. The group-key facts are `[confirm]` against the primary specification, and the abstract group-key requirement this rests on, including the no-shared-signature-key-across-a-principal's-clients property as requirement K5, is consolidated in §10.2, where MLS is positioned as the reference realization rather than the requirement.

Because there is no central authority in a center-free system, the **principal key acts as its own certificate authority for its clients and devices**: the principal signs each client's credential, and a verifier accepts "this client is principal P" by validating that credential chain. Adding a device or a client is the deliberate, high-trust act, for example scanning a code, that the principal then attests, which is exactly the trust binding of Part 1 §2.0, where the human act binds existing trust to a key and the credential records it.

A direct consequence for ordering, and a load-bearing instance of Part 1 §2.0.1 that time is not a fact: the **logical clock is per client and strictly logical, never a wall-clock.** Each client advances only its own `lamport` counter, so there are no cross-client collisions and no coordination is required, and a wall-clock is never consulted because it is an uncorroborable assertion that could not order what must converge. A principal's stream is the **deterministic fold-time merge** across its clients' streams, ordered by lamport and then a stable cryptographic tiebreak, never by time. `Design.`

### 4.6. Canonical on the wire, free in local storage

Three representation decisions follow from one principle, that agreement is on facts and their hashes and nothing more. The bytes that are hashed, signed, and reconciled are fixed by the protocol; everything downstream of a verified fact is a local choice. Getting the first half wrong fails silently, and the second half is where the local-first advantage lives.

**Facts are encoded canonically, and any JSON rendering is only a lens.** A fact **MUST** be encoded in a canonical, deterministic binary form (dag-cbor) for hashing and signing, and a JSON or other human-facing rendering **MUST NOT** be the artifact that is hashed or signed, because hashing and signing require a single reproducible byte sequence and JSON does not provide one: its map-key ordering is unspecified, whitespace is free, number formatting varies, and string escaping and Unicode normalization differ across encoders, so the same logical fact serializes differently, yields a different FactId, and breaks signature verification even though its meaning never changed. That is cross-node divergence entering through a side door, the same error class as ordering by wall-clock or deriving causality from a local index: a value that must be identical across nodes is allowed to vary. dag-cbor closes it because it specifies map-key ordering, a single number model, and a deterministic encoding, so a given fact has exactly one valid byte sequence and one FactId on every node. `Established` (the determinism is a defined property of dag-cbor), and `Verified` by ecosystem use (ATProto encodes its records in dag-cbor and IPLD uses it as a canonical codec). The split to hold onto is that the canonical form is hashed, signed, stored, and reconciled, while the presentation form is computed from it as a lens, never hashed or signed, and free to differ. `Design`, resting on that `Established` determinism. The exact canonical content-id encoding is `[gates-release]` (§4.2).

**The causal reference is one committed reference on the wire, and the DAG is rebuilt locally.** A fact's causal reference **MUST** be the observed governance frontier, a single reference that transitively commits to the fact's entire causal past the way a Merkle root commits to a whole tree (§7.3.1, §7.5.2), and a bare within-branch parent-pointer **MUST NOT** stand in for it, because a bare parent authenticates only author-local order and discards cross-author causality, whether this author's fact was made in knowledge of another's, which is exactly what the fold needs to order concurrents and what no local index can recover once it was never attested. The per-branch sequence of §4.3 carries that author-local order and is additional to this reference, never a substitute for it. The reference is deliberately a single committed hash rather than an explicit multi-hash frontier listing every head a fact saw, which trades one property for another: the committed form is smaller on the wire and preserves full causal fidelity but makes concurrency a *derived* property, computed by resolving two facts' references against a local DAG rather than read off a fact directly, while an explicit frontier is self-describing but larger. Local-first settles the trade toward the committed form, because a node maintains a rebuildable, integrity-checkable DAG index (forward and backward edges, a frontier index, reverse indexes) as a pure local acceleration, so it pays that index cost once and thereafter answers walk-the-graph and what-is-concurrent-with-this as a cheap local lookup, while an explicit frontier would pay off only when reasoning about concurrency without such an index, chiefly during initial sync. `Design.`

**The reconciliation format is fixed; local storage and derived views are free.** Because agreement is on facts and their hashes and not on any storage layout, the wire format and each node's local format are decoupled, which a shared-state chain cannot do, since there every node reads one structure and the representation must agree. The reconciliation format is fixed by the protocol, signed facts in canonical dag-cbor carrying the committed causal reference above (§7.3.1), and it **MUST** agree across nodes because the fold consumes it. Local storage is whatever each node chooses: a node **MAY** prune raw facts behind a verified checkpoint (§6, §7.3.7), keep only the folded-now plus a tail, and store in any structure it likes, because a dropped fact is re-requestable and re-verifiable against its signature, so storage depth is bounded only by how much history the node wishes to serve. Local consumption is equally free: a node **MAY** build any number of derived views (materialized tables, reverse indexes, the DAG index above) as integrity-checkable, rebuildable caches, none authoritative on its own, since any one can be rebuilt from the chain and re-verified if suspect. Three efficiencies are therefore three independent dials: the protocol fixes the wire, and each node trades its own disk and CPU against query speed and against how much it is willing to serve, with integrity guaranteed throughout because everything traces to signed facts. `Design.`

## 5. Identity, principals, rights, roles, and capabilities

`Realizes: P-Peer-Equality, P-Local-Truth, P-Knowable-Truth, P-Durable-Enablement`

This section fixes the vocabulary the rest of the part runs on, and it is the section a reviewer presses hardest, because it is where `P-Peer-Equality` is enforced by mechanism rather than assumed. It asks one precise question, *in what ways may one persona differ from another?*, and answers with **exactly four properties, two necessarily equal and two legitimately unequal**, then separates the **identity layer** (principals and clients) from the **governance layer** (personae and weight). It depends on §4's identifiers and is orthogonal to how bytes travel in §6, since standing does not depend on transport.

**Provenance of this model.** The equality framing and the principal, persona, and client vocabulary are Drystone's own synthesis, not sourced from an external spec, while two prior-art vocabularies are adopted as-is rather than renamed so the design does not fight the systems it builds on. MLS terms, client, member, leaf, Authentication Service, and Delivery Service, keep their RFC 9420 and RFC 9750 meanings (§10.2). Meadowcap's term **capability** is kept verbatim, and Drystone does not reuse it for anything else; the Willow Meadowcap specification defines the concept as "an unforgeable token that bestows read or write access for some data to a particular person, issued by the owner of that data" (§5.5, §10.4). So the property nouns sit at distinct planes, **resource** (a device fact, §5.4), **Group Role** (in-Group governance authority, §5.5), and **capability** (data access, beneath Group Roles), and none collides with the prior art. Lowercase **role** is the genus, the category of delegated authority, and a **Group Role** is a concrete grant inside a Group (§5.0, Part 1 §2.3).

### 5.0. Two equalities, two inequalities, and the two layers

The question `P-Peer-Equality` answers is *in what ways may one persona differ from another?* There are exactly four properties, and the model is that **two are necessarily equal and two are legitimately unequal.** "Personae are equal" is shorthand for this four-way split, and collapsing it into one phrase obscures which axes are equal and which are not.

**Total equality is not the goal, and could not be, which is what makes this an axis question rather than a yes-or-no one.** Two entities equal on *every* axis are indiscernible, and two indiscernible entities are one entity, not two, so "make the peers totally equal" does not describe two equal peers; it dissolves the distinctness that makes them two. Distinctness has to live on some axis, and here it lives on identity: two personae are always distinct lineages (§5.2). That is what frees the governance axes to be assigned deliberately, because the personae stay two however those axes fall. So "why not total equality?" is not a rhetorical objection to wave off but a methodological question the model must resolve: on which axes must personae be equal for peer standing to be real, and on which is difference legitimate or unavoidable? The four-property split is that resolution, and it is forced rather than chosen. Equality is load-bearing on rights and weight, because equal voice and equal count are what peer standing *is*, so those are held equal by mechanism. Difference is legitimate on Group Roles, since a grant is the whole point of delegation, and unavoidable on resources, a device fact no protocol can level, so those are left free. Peer-equality is equality on the standing axes, held there precisely because distinctness is already guaranteed on identity and difference on the remaining axes then costs nothing.

What makes the equality matter is what a **persona** is: the **human layer's manifestation** in the system, a principal by virtue of its key pair, present through lineage and verification (§4.5), not a node or a device. So peer-equality is **equality of personae as represented**, equality in expression and in count: the protocol guarantees by mechanism that one recognized persona carries equal rights and one flat unit of weight. Whether a persona corresponds to one distinct human is a separate question the protocol does **not** answer and **could not**, because that binding has no technical representation to read (§5.2), so there is no fact to certify; it is a social-utility judgment the Group makes at its own standard (Part 1 §2.0, §5.6). The mechanical guarantee is what makes that judgment meaningful, and does not substitute for it. That is the razor (Part 1 §2.0) applied to the persona concept itself.

**The two equalities**, equal for every persona, always:

- **Right, what a principal inherently holds.** The floor of voice, tenure, and exit or fork (§5.3), **equal for every persona and unremovable.** The proof that it is a right and not a Group Role is that the last of them, exit or fork, survives even when every Group Role is stripped and even when a quorum captures the Group: participation persists as the standing to leave with your state and continue. A right is precisely the thing that cannot be delegated or revoked, standing in the system rather than in any one Group, and it attaches to the **principal** and flows to its clients.

- **Weight, how much a persona counts in governance.** **Flat, one per distinct persona**, where a persona is the unit a Group's members resolve to by lineage (§4.5), regardless of how many clients, devices, resources, or Group Roles carry that lineage. Weight is equal **not by a separate decree but as a consequence of the equal right**: if standing-to-participate is equal, then standing-to-be-counted is equal, the second being the governance image of the first rather than an independent conclusion. It attaches to the **persona** (§5.6). A Group recognizes its **members**, which are clients in MLS terms (§5.2); lineage resolves those member-clients to **one persona** (§4.5), and it is that resolved persona that is counted once. The system attests the resolution by provenance; what it does **not** attest is whether a persona is a distinct **person**, which is a contextual judgment the Group makes at its own confidence (§5.6), and is why this part avoids "personhood-verified," since "verified" would imply the system did the verifying.

**The two inequalities**, legitimately different between personae:

- **Resource, what a node has.** Storage, uptime, reachability, a push token, a radio, a willingness to relay. A property of the **device or node**, not of identity: **intrinsic, descriptive, expected to be unequal, and not delegable**, since you cannot hand another node your RAM. It is a fact about every node (a persona's clients, but also meers and relays, §5.4), listed among the persona inequalities because *across personae* it is one of the two ways they legitimately differ. A resource says what is *possible*, never what is *permitted* and never how much a persona *counts* (§5.4). The word is **resource**, not "capability," because "capability" is Meadowcap's word for data access (§5.5) and "resource" names the device fact without the false slide from "able to" to "entitled to."

- **Group Role, what governance authority a principal has been granted.** Admin, moderator, gating, the act-for-the-Group authority, the authority to issue capabilities (§5.5). A Group Role is **granted by member consent, scoped, attenuating, and always revocable**, the one *operational* inequality the design permits: personae may hold different Group Roles, and that is normal. Crucially a Group Role **rides entirely above the two equalities**, so granting or revoking one never changes a persona's rights floor or its unit of weight. Group Roles are the application-layer construct MLS deliberately leaves undefined (§5.5).

The one-sentence model: **personae are equal in rights, and therefore equal in weight, and unequal in resources and revocable Group Roles.** Rights do not vary; Group Roles do. The inequality people sometimes imagine when they picture rights differing is always a *Group Role*, a grant, never a right.

**Capability is not a fifth property.** The Meadowcap data-access grant to read or write an area of a namespace (§5.5) is the **mechanism a Group Role operates through**, not a fifth persona-property: a Group Role may carry the authority to *issue* capabilities, and the capabilities themselves are data-plane tokens (§7.1, §10.4), one level below the question of how personae differ. It sits *under* Group Roles, not beside resources.

The four properties sit across two layers, because the entity that holds rights is not the same granularity as the device that acts. The **identity layer** is **principals and clients**: a principal is the permission-holding entity, one key-lineage, realized by one or more clients across one or more devices. The **governance layer** is **personae and weight**: a persona is the human layer's manifestation, the entity rights and weight attach to, and the locus at which the social-utility calls the system cannot compute (Part 1 §2.0) are adjudicated. Both are defined in full in §5.2; what matters here is that they are distinct, the identity layer answering *who is acting and can it be authenticated*, the governance layer answering *whose standing counts and by how much*.

### 5.1. The only canonical state is local

A client can prove exactly one thing, its own local state, and its local store is **canonical for that principal**. Any belief about another principal's state is **comparative** (range reconciliation) or **asserted** (a signed fact it accepted), never canonical, and there is **no global canonical state** by design. A lagging client computes a stale-but-honest state, never a false one, because it is only ever reading its own store. `Verified`, and it is the property §7 relies on to make authority a fold over an append-only view.

### 5.2. Principal, client, persona: the identity model

The consolidated term lattice and the invariants of record are in Appendix D; this section is the prose source those entries summarize.

A **principal** is a **permission-holding entity, identified by one key-lineage.** This is the genus, defined by its *identity* (one authenticatable lineage) rather than merely by its function, so that "holds a permission" does not collapse into "anything at all." A principal is reasoned about through the permissions it carries, and "permission" spans planes: a Group member holds **Group Roles** (in-Group governance authority, §5.5), while a non-Group principal like a meer holds **ecosystem permissions** (connectivity and delivery reach, §5.4) and no Group Role. The kinds of principal:

- a **persona**, the principal that **manifests a human**, a principal by virtue of its key pair, carrying the rights floor and one unit of weight, and the locus at which the social-utility calls the system cannot compute (Part 1 §2.0) are adjudicated because a person stands behind it, the common case being one person, one persona per Group, possibly many devices. Its clients run on its devices, tied to the persona by lineage (§4.5), and the persona is neither a client nor a device nor any node.

- a **Group**, a collective that can hold a Group Role as a single principal, its identity model an **open seam** (§5.10).

- a **delegate**, not a separate species but a **state**: a persona or Group currently holding a Group Role delegated by another principal (§5.5).

A **meer** is **not** a principal in the Group-governance sense and does not appear above: it is a broad-plane principal holding ecosystem permissions (§5.4), a blind store-and-forward node offering availability capacity, configured by a persona to serve ciphertext. It holds no Group Role, no right, and no weight, and it is named by persona-level configuration rather than enrolled as an identity. It is defined in §5.4 and realized at the transport layer in §6.

A **client** is **software on a device that is a member of a Group**, one MLS **leaf**, one **signature key**, one **credential**, authenticated as a **member** via the **AS** (§10.2). The term is MLS's, kept for consistency. A **device** is hardware (a node, §5.4) and may host **more than one client**, and a human may have **more than one device**, so the hosting chain is human, then devices, then clients, and a principal is **realized by one or more clients across one or more devices.** MLS addresses clients; Drystone governance addresses principals, folding a principal's clients and devices by lineage to one persona (§4.5).

**The governance-integrity spine is identity, not client count.** Governance quorums and thresholds count **personae**, resolved by lineage to one persona per rooting key pair (§4.5), **never clients**, so many clients across several devices are one identity's worth of standing. This is what makes "you cannot buy your way to shifting the center of gravity" structurally true: adding clients or devices adds **resources**, never **rights** and never **weight**, because the count is over personae, so a persona with one device and a persona with five are weighted identically. This is the property whose absence made early crypto-governance takeovers possible and painful to unwind, and Drystone makes identity-not-resource the basis of weight by construction.

**The keystone distinction: a lineage is a provenance object, a persona is the human it manifests, and personhood is a social judgment.** These are different *kinds* of thing, and keeping them distinct is the identity-layer instance of the founding provenance-versus-utility split (Part 1 §2.0). A **lineage** is **technically representable**, a cryptographic-provenance chain the protocol can point at, verify signatures against, and count, delivered with certainty. A **persona** is the **human that lineage is taken to manifest**, and whether a given lineage corresponds to a distinct person, the one-persona-one-human binding, has **no technical representation at all**, because it was never a fact the system holds; it is a judgment the *group* makes (§5.6), where to *recognize* is to decide to **treat** a lineage as a distinct person for the group's own purposes, never to *verify* it. The system counts lineages, the group decides which lineages it recognizes as distinct persons, and that recognition is what turns a counted lineage into a weighted persona. So the binding, *this lineage is one person*, is **not a lookup but an adjudication**, which is precisely why it is a seat of social-utility judgment rather than something the part resolves. Collapsing persona into personhood is the same category error as "the network can certify truth," asking a provenance system to deliver a utility verdict, which is why "persona" and "personhood" are separate words here and the separation is load-bearing.

**Open seam: the principal that anchors a multi-client lineage.** The cross-device identity is the **principal**, and the Group-as-principal identity takes a concrete shape in §5.10, a Meadowcap **communal namespace** rather than a derived central credential; what is designed-not-frozen is its key establishment and rotation under membership change, carried to Appendix B.

*Running example: Alice and Bob are personae, principals that hold standing, and each of Alice's two clients carries a membership key descending from her single lineage (beat E1).*

### 5.3. Rights: the inherent, equal floor, never delegated, never unequal

A right is what a principal **inherently holds**, not what it may be *granted*, which is a Group Role (§5.5). The floor is held **identically by every persona** and **cannot** be delegated away or stripped without degrading the system (Part 1 §2.4), and it is one of the two equalities of §5.0. The base floor:

- **Read your own local history.** Unqualified, identical for every persona.

- **Read the history of a Group you are a member of, for the period of your membership.** It begins at join and ends at leave, includes what the persona was present for, does not extend to content authored after the persona leaves, and does not retroactively vanish for content the persona legitimately held while a member (§5.7).

- **A Group holds full history for itself,** independent of any member's tenure.

Two consequences keep the floor precise. **A persona's own history is permanent, while its window into a shared Group is bounded by membership**, two different histories treated as such. And **a principal with no local history of its own still holds the full read-your-own-history right**, exercised over an empty set, so a persona that has joined but authored nothing satisfies the right vacuously. A meer is not the example here, because it is infrastructure and not a principal, so it holds no rights at all (§5.4); where a principal is *blind*, that is the absence of a key and of any Group Role conferring read, never a restriction on a right.

**One open check before the rights set hardens** (carried from Part 1 §2.4): the proven floor in this draft is the read-rights triple above, while the fuller articulation is **three rights**, **tenure** (standing to remain a persona), **voice** (standing to assert into the record and be corroborated or refuted), and **exit** (the right to fork), each fixed by what its removal would foreclose. A claim on a Group's commons is **not** a right, because it is not part of the inalienable floor; where it has substance it is a data-layer matter, ownership of a Meadowcap communal namespace (§5.10), handled there. The remaining open check is **tenure under re-key**: whether the §7 survivor or re-key path can leave a persona formally a member but unable to re-establish its standing after a re-key, in which case tenure is not yet a clean right and the set cannot harden, so it needs a concrete test. `Design` (Appendix B).

*Running example: Bob holds exactly this floor, identical to Alice's, even though Alice also holds a moderator Group Role Set, since the Set sits above the floor and cannot reach into it (beat E1).*

### 5.4. Resources: node facilities, descriptive, not delegable

A **resource** is what a **node has**, a facility intrinsic to the hardware and its configured intent. Resources are a property of **any node in the distributed system**, not only of a persona's clients: a persona's client devices have resources, and so do meers (the blind store-and-forward nodes below) and relays. The persona-versus-meer distinction is drawn in the **identity** model (§5.2), never in resources, because a resource is a physical fact about a box, blind to whether that box is an identity. Resources are **descriptive** (they report what is possible), **unequal across nodes**, and **not delegable**, since a node cannot hand another node its storage, uptime, or radio. A resource enables a principal to *fulfil* a Group Role (§5.5) or makes it *unsuited* to one, but holding a resource is never itself a grant of authority and never adds governance weight.

**Common resources** (the current set, not closed):

- **Availability capacity.** The device can hold and serve a Group's *encrypted* objects, including buffering for offline members. Availability capacity does **not** imply read, since such a device holds ciphertext it cannot decrypt because it is never given a key. A node a persona configures to do only this, blindly, is a **meer** (below), which is infrastructure, not a principal.

- **Read and search-offload capacity.** The device can decrypt, index, retain, and serve a Group's history, which is the *facility*; whether the principal is *permitted* to is a read Group Role (§5.5).

- **Reachability.** The device is positioned, by public reachability and uptime, to forward for others.

The pairing rule, for the resources that matter to governance, namely a principal's own clients: a **Group Role** (the governance authority, §5.5) is only useful to a principal whose **client** has the **resource** to exercise it, so granting a read Group Role to a device with no decryption capacity is inert. Group Roles and resources are matched at the **Group Role Set** layer (§5.5), which is exactly why a Group Role Set bundles a role set *with an expectation of resources*. The anti-capture consequence is in the words: a node may have more resources, and that buys it no rights and no weight, only the ability to be *useful*, never to *count for more*.

**The meer is blind store-and-forward infrastructure.** "Meer" is a short name for a blind store-and-forward node, not a distinct model entity. Such a node accepts, retains, and serves a Group's encrypted objects, seeing ciphertext plus routing metadata only. It is **not a Group principal, member, or persona**, holds **no Group Role**, **no rights floor**, and **no weight**, and it is not enrolled as an identity. Its blindness is **structural**, since it is never issued a decryption key, so there is no "decrypt" to forbid and no Group Role to strip, and a Tier-0 meer can prove it holds zero payload keys (§8).

**A meer's presence in a scope is a fabric-level fact; a persona's *use* of it is a per-persona decision**, and the two layers must not be collapsed. A meer participates in a Group's delivery scope the way any swarm node does, in the gossip fabric, carrying and seeing the sealed envelope and its routing metadata as it passes, and that presence is not something a single persona can revoke, because it is a property of the **Delivery Fabric** (the blind carrying population, §6.3), not a per-persona grant. What *is* a per-persona decision is whether to **use and rely on** the meer, calling in for held messages, depending on it for durability, or declining all of it. Because a single meer's exposure sits at the fabric level and is shared across everyone in scope, and a meer can be in scope for several Groups at once since scope is exposure reach and not bounded by any one Group's membership, one persona declining to interact does not remove the meer from scope or change anyone else's exposure. So the meer is not adopted per persona: its scope presence is a fabric fact, and only the trust-and-use decision is the individual's.

**A meer is a principal in the broad sense, though not in the Group-governance sense, and it does hold permissions.** Saying the meer holds no Group Role, no rights, and no weight is a claim about the *Group-governance plane* only, and does not mean the meer is authority-less in the system. The meer is a **principal** in the broad sense of §5.2, a principal reasoned about through the permissions it carries, and those permissions are real and enumerable, just of a different kind: **ecosystem permissions**, connectivity and delivery reach rather than in-Group governance. A meer typically holds permission to be in a Group's delivery swarm, and often permission to talk to a downstream push notifier, which in turn may hold permission to talk to an external third party such as an OS push service. This chain of ecosystem permissions is what lets the meer function as infrastructure, and it is why Group Role, right, and weight are reserved for the governance plane while permission spans planes: the meer holds ecosystem permissions and no Group-governance ones. These permissions are **enumerable** and **blind**, since exercising them never confers sight of content.

**Two revocation planes, and why a persona's recourse against a meer lives on the second.** Revocation is not one mechanism; it acts at two layers with different principals and different authority.

- **Group-governance revocation** (§5.7, §5.8) acts *inside* a Group, on Group facts, a Group Role or membership. It runs through the Group's replicated policy and the k-of-n threshold counted per persona, and it is global to the Group by construction: once the fold accepts it, the fact holds for every member.

- **Node-local withdrawal of use** acts at the layer of the software an individual persona runs as a standalone authoritative node. It is how a persona withdraws its own reliance on a *non-Group* helper like a meer or relay: it stops calling the helper, stops depending on it for durability, declines to interact. Its authority comes from the standalone-authoritative-node premise (§5.1), so it needs no threshold and no governance round. This is **not** removal of the helper from scope, since the meer remains in the Delivery Fabric and still sees whatever routing metadata the fabric exposes; it is the **exit right exercised at the client level** (§5.9), a recognition of the persona's autonomy over its own node.

The **asymmetry is the tell** that these are two planes. A Group Role revocation is global to the Group and changes a Group fact, while a node-local withdrawal is local to the one persona and changes only that persona's behavior, leaving the helper in scope. Whether to withdraw, and the shape of the response, re-homing durability to a different meer, leaning on more than one, pulling the function onto your own device, or forking the Group entirely if the concern is Group-wide, is a **social-utility judgment** about trust and tradeoffs rather than a mechanical toggle, which is why the protocol makes the response available but does not compute it (the Part 1 §2.0 razor, one layer down).

**Helper governance and alignment is a first-class concern, treated separately.** Because scope is broader than a Group and a single helper can be in scope for many Groups, the operation and governance of helper nodes, that is meers, relays, and push notifiers, meaningfully shapes the scope of exposure across the whole system, at a layer no individual persona's non-interaction can dissolve. A persona's node-local withdrawal is the individual backstop, the exit exercised at the client, and it is not a substitute for the question of who runs these helpers and whether they are ideologically and operationally aligned. That question is a first-class concern in its own right, not something each Group settles internally, and it is carried as an open item (Appendix B and the conventions reference).

**A meer is optional, not required.** Clients communicate directly peer-to-peer over the transport (§6), so two clients, or many, can exchange MLS messages with no meer in the path. What a meer adds is **offline persistence**: because MLS operates asynchronously (RFC 9750 §6.3: "No operation in MLS requires two distinct clients or members to be online simultaneously"), a message for an absent recipient has to be held somewhere, and a meer is the optional augmentation that holds it, useful when some node has high-availability resources to spare. In MLS terms a meer is one realization of the **Delivery Service's store-and-forward function** (§10.2), and MLS does not require a central Delivery Service, since clients on a peer-to-peer network supply the delivery logic themselves. **Distinguish the meer from an iroh relay** (§6): the relay is a transport-layer blind packet forwarder for reachability and NAT traversal, holding nothing, while the meer is an application-layer store that persists encrypted objects for later delivery. Both are blind, they sit at different layers, and neither is mandatory.

*Running example: Carol's node has vast resources, highly available with deep storage, while Bob's phone has little, and the difference is descriptive and confers neither of them any standing (beat E3).*

### 5.5. Group Role, capability, Group Role Set, and delegation: the governance and data-access planes

Two distinct kinds of grant sit above the rights floor, at two different planes, and a third construct bundles them. None touches the inherent rights floor or the flat weight.

- **Group Role, an in-Group governance authority.** A scoped, attenuable authority to *act* in the Group's governance: admit or remove members, gate distribution, hold the **act-for-the-Group** authority (§5.10), or *issue and revoke capabilities* over the Group's data. A Group Role is **granted to a principal** by member consent, is **revocable** (§5.7), composes by union, and mutates freely, so granting or revoking one is ordinary governance traffic and no alarm, and each grant is a governance fact (§7). **This is the layer MLS deliberately leaves to the application.** RFC 9750 §6.4 states that MLS "does not itself enforce any access control on group operations," and places access-control policy at the application layer instead, noting for instance that an application restricting membership changes to an administrator must itself make that policy, and the administrator's identity, known to members (RFC 9750 §6.4; §10.2). *Drystone's synthesis, marked as our reading of how that clause is discharged, not the RFC's words:* the Group Role layer **is** that application policy, enforced not by a server but by the governance fold, so a child's `Remove(parent)` is a well-formed MLS message that honest members **reject as unauthorized** because the replicated Group Role policy does not grant the child that authority. The honest seam is that the protection is convergent agreement that the operation is unauthorized, not cryptographic impossibility of emitting it (§5.7). `Verified-RFC` for the quoted MLS access-control position; `Design` for the Group Role reading.

- **Capability, a data-access grant, Meadowcap's term kept verbatim.** The Willow Meadowcap specification defines a capability as "an unforgeable token that bestows read or write access for some data to a particular person, issued by the owner of that data," and adds that a capability also bestows the ability to mint further capabilities for the same data, each restrictable to a subset. A capability is about **reading and writing entries** and nothing else: it does not admit members, does not gate, and carries no vote. Capabilities are *issued under* a Group Role, the authority to issue them, and live in the data plane (§7.1, §10.4). Drystone keeps Meadowcap's term because it intends Meadowcap, or a Meadowcap-shaped mechanism, as the data-access realization, and renaming it would fight the prior art. `Established` for the Meadowcap primitive, verified against the Willow Meadowcap specification; `Design` for the Drystone binding.

- **Group Role Set, a named, pinned, Group-recognized bundle** of Group Roles, the capabilities they imply, and the **resources expected to fulfil them**, with an **enforced composition**: a **required** set, a **forbidden** set, and optionally **mutually-exclusive** Group Roles that may never travel together. It serves two functions. It lets a Group **grant or revoke a bundle as one unit** rather than Group-Role-by-Group-Role, the human-fatigue reason, since people reason about "moderator" and not a list of individual grants. And it lets the Group **constrain composition** for separation of powers, so a holder of one Set may be forbidden a given Group Role. It is prescriptive, answering what a principal of this name is supposed to hold and never hold, and drift from the pinned composition is an integrity event the Group flags. `Design`; the name and the two functions are fixed, and the fuller mechanism develops across §5.5 to §5.9.

**Delegation** is the act of granting a Group Role or passing on a capability, with two normative properties.

- **Always attenuating and bounded.** A principal **MUST** be able to delegate only a subset of what it holds and never a superset, because a principal that could delegate more than it holds would mint authority from nothing, letting a permission appear without the consent every grant requires (`Realizes: P-Peer-Equality`; the attenuation requirement, §7.2 R2; this is also Meadowcap's confinement property for capabilities). Delegation **moves or narrows** authority and never widens or mints it.

- **Two targets, one primitive.** A principal **MAY** delegate to another client in its **own principal's device Group**, keeping trust within personal control, or to another principal in the **shared Group**, a cooperative anchor such as another member's highly-available node. It is the same mechanism, and the trust boundary is the user's choice, which is what lets Drystone refuse consolidation onto a single keeper.

All of these live in the **grant planes** and **none alters weight or the rights floor**: a principal carrying any Group Role or capability still holds its complete inherent floor and its single unit of weight. The mechanical check is that every Group Role Set **MUST** be definable as `floor + [explicit Group Role set] + [implied capabilities] + [expected resources]`, and a name meaning "entitled to fewer **rights**" is **forbidden** because that would be a smuggled rights distinction. **Rights have no presets; Group Roles, capabilities, and Group Role Sets do.**

A Group Role Set's pinning is enforced by a **drift check**: the Group gathers the Group Role grants in force for a principal from the governance log and compares them against the declared Set's required, forbidden, and mutually-exclusive composition, and a mismatch in **any** direction is the alarm, whether a principal *acquired* a forbidden Group Role (dangerous), *lost* a required one (failing the job relied on), or *combined* two mutually-exclusive Group Roles (a restricted combination). The check is mechanical because every side is a fact already in the log. Separately, "this node is blind" is trustworthy for a structural reason rather than a pinned Group Role set, since a Tier-0 store node can prove it holds zero payload keys (§8).

**Trust-dynamics changes fail loud, by design.** Two cases route to a noisy hard-stop rather than a silent rejection. The first is a governance action placing two mutually-exclusive Group Roles on one principal. The second is a reconfiguration that would make a Group's blind store-and-forward node, a meer (§5.4), receive decryption keys, converting blind infrastructure into a reading party. In either case the action surfaces to the affected Group, "a restricted change was attempted, your communications may be at risk, fork without the principals who voted it?", and routes to human adjudication (§7.6). The reasoning is that a quorum voting to de-blind a node everyone relied on as blind has *changed the trust dynamics of the Group*, which is precisely the kind of standing contradiction that is a utility judgment and not a computation. `Design`, decided; the loud-failure rung ties to §7.6.

Worked example, a **moderator** (a Group Role Set):

```
moderator (a Group Role Set) ::= floor                            // full inherent rights, unchanged
                        + requires role { admit, remove }     // governance authority granted by consent
                        + expects  resource { reachability }  // device facts that help fulfil it
                        + forbids  role { act-for-the-Group }  // kept separate from Group-signing authority
                        + holds    capability { }              // no standing data-access grant by default
```

A moderator is a **persona** holding a moderation Group Role: its rights floor and unit of weight are unchanged by the grant, and revoking the Group Role returns it to a bare persona. The Set's pinning makes drift an integrity event, so acquiring the forbidden `act-for-the-Group` Group Role, or losing a required one, is flagged, and deleting the Set name changes nothing about any persona's rights. `Design` for the Group Role Set drift-check and mutual-exclusion formalism.

A **meer** is *not* a Group Role Set, because it is not a principal in the Group-governance sense: it is blind store-and-forward infrastructure a persona configures for its own durability (§5.4), with no Group Role to pin and no rights to bundle.

*Running example: Alice delegates Carol's node a read capability, a subset of what she holds and never a superset, so the grant moves access without minting authority (beat E3).*

#### 5.5.1. The general delegate primitive: one mechanism under recovery, escalation, C2, failover, and revocation

The delegation of §5.5 is the routine, always-present case: a live principal narrows a Group Role or capability it holds and passes the subset to another live principal.
